text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 15.35pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 0in;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">                                                     Children Act, 1974</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.35pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 15.35pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 0in;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 66(1)(2)— Presumption as to the age of the accused—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.35pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 15.35pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 0in;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">When enquiry to ascertain age of a child can be resorted to—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.35pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 15.35pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 66(2) of the Children Act, 1974 empowers the court to presume as to the age of the accused. If the court draws presumption by physical appearance of the accused that he is not a child below 16 years of age, the court is not required to resort to enquiry for ascertaining the age of the accused. Only when the court finds the accused to be child below 16 years it shall then resort to an enquiry to ascertain the age of the child accused as contemplated under sub-section (1) of section 66 of the Children Act, 1974.<em>Anwarul Islam Vs. The State, 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 18.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; text-indent: 9.1pt; line-height: 12.0pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">                                               Children Act, 1974</span></strong> </span></p><p> </p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 15.6pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Trial of Juvenile offender—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.35pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 15.35pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">When the offender is a child below 16 years of age he has to be tried under the Children Act, 1974. Court is under a duty in such situation to undertake enquiry to ascertain the age of the accused, otherwise the trial of a child Linder the common law will be without jurisdiction rendering the conviction and sentence a nullity. <em>Monir Hossain (Md.) Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (HC) 275.</em></span></p><p> </p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; text-indent: 9.1pt; line-height: 12.0pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></p><p> </p></div></div>

5.4pt;	mso-para-margin-top:0in;	mso-para-margin-right:0in;	mso-para-margin-bottom:10.0pt;	mso-para-margin-left:0in;	line-height:115%;	mso-pagination:widow-orphan;	font-size:11.0pt;	mso-bidi-font-size:14.0pt;	font-family:"Calibri","sans-serif";	mso-ascii-font-family:Calibri;	mso-ascii-theme-font:minor-latin;	mso-hansi-font-family:Calibri;	mso-hansi-theme-font:minor-latin;}</style><![endif]--></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-right: 0.25pt; line-height: 14.15pt; text-align: center;"><span style="font-family: times new roman,times,serif;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11pt;">Ain Sringkhola Bighnakari Aparadh Daman (Druto Bichar) Ain, 2002</span></strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-right: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 14.15pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 19.7pt;"><span style="font-family: times new roman,times,serif;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11pt;">Section HKM and <span style="letter-spacing: -1pt;">4(1)-</span> Extortion of money under threat to life at gun point—</span></strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-right: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 14.15pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 19.7pt;"><span style="font-size: 11pt; font-family: times new roman,times,serif;">When the charge brought against the convict-petitioner was duly established by consistent <strong><em><span style="letter-spacing: 1pt;">and </span></em></strong><span style="letter-spacing: 1pt;">reliable evidence together with long </span>absconsion <span style="letter-spacing: 1pt;">of the </span>together porting at the guilt at the convict-petitioner and the trial court as well as the appellate court concurrently having found the convict-petitioner guilty of the commission of the offence convicting and awarding the sentence which was duly affirmed by the appellate court, the Revisional court found nothing to interfere therewith.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-right: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 14.15pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 19.7pt;"><span style="font-size: 11pt; font-family: times new roman,times,serif;">Although absconsion by itself is no evidence and it can not be the sole basis of conviction, long absconsion from immediately after the commission of the offence offers sufficiant corroboration to other evidence forming the basis of conviction. <em>Rom Bepari Vs. The State, 10 MLR (2005) (HC) 381.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-right: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 14.15pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 19.7pt;"><span style="font-family: times new roman,times,serif;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11pt;">Ain Srinkhola Bignakari Aparadh Daman (Druta Bicher) Ain 2002—</span></strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-right: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 14.15pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 19.7pt;"><span style="font-family: times new roman,times,serif;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11pt;">Section-4 Offence Kidnapping and putting the victim in illegal confinement for realisation of ransom under thereat of death—</span></strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-right: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 14.15pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 19.7pt;"><span style="font-family: times new roman,times,serif;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11pt;">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section <span style="letter-spacing: 0.5pt;">154</span>— <span style="letter-spacing: 0.5pt;">FIR is no</span> substantive piece <span style="letter-spacing: 1.5pt;">of</span> <span style="letter-spacing: 0.5pt;">evidence-</span></span></strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-right: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 14.15pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 19.7pt;"><span style="font-family: times new roman,times,serif;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11pt;">Evidence of related witness cannot be discarded when they appear to be natural—</span></strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-right: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 14.15pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 19.7pt;"><span style="font-size: 11pt; font-family: times new roman,times,serif;">It is well settled that FIR is not a substantive piece of evidence. It is important that it gives the information about the occurrence first in point of time and can be taken into consideration to check subsequent embellishment of the prosecution case.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-right: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 14.15pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 19.7pt;"><span style="font-size: 11pt; font-family: times new roman,times,serif;">When the witnesses who are related to each other appear to be natural to the occurrence and their evidences appear to be reliable, such evidence can well form the basis of conviction. When the prosecution appears to have proved the charge by consistent and reliable evidence the conviction and sentence passed by the trial court thereon and affirmed by the lower appellate court are held perfectly justified by the High Court Division.<em>Sohel (Md.) Vs. The State, 10 MLR (2005) (HC) 239.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-right: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 14.15pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 19.7pt;"><span style="font-family: times new roman,times,serif;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11pt;">Ain Srinkhola Bighnakari Aparadh Daman (Druto Bicher) Ain, 2002—</span></strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-right: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 14.15pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 19.7pt;"><span style="font-family: times new roman,times,serif;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11pt;">Section 4(1)— Charge of the offence can well be framed when primafacie case is found established on the materials on record—</span></strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-right: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 14.15pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 19.7pt;"><span style="font-family: times new roman,times,serif;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11pt;">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898—</span></strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-right: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 14.15pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 19.7pt;"><span style="font-family: times new roman,times,serif;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11pt;">Section 241A— Accused can not be discharged when the allegations are supported by materials on record—</span></strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-right: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 14.15pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 19.7pt;"><span style="font-size: 11pt; font-family: times new roman,times,serif;">Trial Court, is quite justified to frame charge under section 4(1) of the Ain Srinkhola Bignakari Aparadh (Druta Bicher) Ain, 2002 when the materials on record prima facie support the case to go for trial. Court is not required to scrutinize the adequacy or inadequacy of the evidence at this stage which can only be done during trial. Having regard to the position of law as above the High Court Division summarily rejected the application under section 435 and 439 Cr.P.C. seeking to interfere with the rejection of the application under section 241A Cr.P.C. <em>Haji Mohammad Selim and others Vs. State, c.</em></span></p></div></div>
2982	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">Anti-Corruption Act, 1957 </h2>

style="margin-right: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 14.15pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 19.7pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Anti-Corruption officer as envisaged under section 3(3) of the Anti-Corruption Act, 1957 is empowered to exercise power of give<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>statement in connection of enquiry. In a preliminary enquiry for ascertaining the prima-facie truth or otherwise of an allegation when the person so directed is not an accused, such direction is not volatile of the provision of article 35(4) of the Constitution. Abu <em>Siddique and another Vs. </em><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;"> </span><em>Government of Bangladesh represented by the Secretary Ministry of Defence and others, </em></span><!-- [if gte mso 9]><xml> <o:OfficeDocumentSettings>  <o:RelyOnVML/>  <o:AllowPNG/> </o:OfficeDocumentSettings></xml><![endif]--><em><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">7 MLR (2002) (AD) 212.</span></em></p><p> </p><p> </p><p style="text-align: center;"> </p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.35pt; line-height: 15.35pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">                                     Anti-Corruption Act, 1957</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.35pt; line-height: 15.35pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 4(1)— Notice calling for property statement<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>pursuant<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>to satisfaction of the Government—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.35pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 15.35pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Notice calling for statement of properties believed to have been acquired disproportionate to known sources of income as envisaged under section 4(1) of the Anti-Corruption </span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Palatino Linotype','serif'; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Palatino Linotype';">Act, 1957 can only be issued pursuant to the satisfaction of the Government and not of the Bureau of Anti-Corruption. Proceedings drawn following the notice not based on satisfaction of the Government are held to be illegal and without jurisdiction and accordingly the conviction and sentence passed therein are set aside.</span> <em><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Palatino Linotype','serif'; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Palatino Linotype';">Saidur Rahman Vs. class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 10.55pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 15.35pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span Roman','serif';">Section 19A— Bail of accused—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 15.35pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Upon rejection of repeated bail petitions fresh bail petitions are permissible. So is the case with appeal against rejection of bail petition—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 15.35pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">                                           Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 15.35pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 498— Grant of bail in non-bailable offence</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">—</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 15.35pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">When investigation could not be completed within the specified time and when the accused is in custody for long time, the accused in such case of non-baible offence may be granted bail as provided under sub-section (4) of section 339C of Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 15.35pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Repeated bail petition is permissible after the rejection of former ones. The same is the position of law with regard to appeal against repeated rejection of bail petition, <em>KhairulAlam (Md.) alias Masum Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (HC) 309.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 12.0pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.35pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 15.35pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 0in;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">                                                          Arms Act, 1878</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.35pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 15.35pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 0in;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 19(A) and 19(f)— Offence are exclusively triable by Special Tribunal under Special Powers Act, 1974 and not by the Jana Nirapatta Bighnakari Aparadh Damon Tribunal.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.35pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 15.35pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Offences under section 19(A) and 19(f) of the Arms Act, 1878 are exclusively triable by Special Tribunal established under the Special Powers Act, 1974. Jana Nirapatta Bighnakari Aparadh Damon Tribunal has no jurisdiction to try such offence. Therefore the offences under Arms Act shall be tried by Special Tribunal and the offence under the Jana Nirapatta (Bishesh Bidhan) Ain, 2000 shall be tried by the Jana Nirapatta Bighnakari Aparadh Damon Tribunal.<em>Abdul Mannan and others Vs. The State, 9 MLR (2004) (HC) 258.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.35pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 15.35pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 0in;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">                                                           Arms Act, 1878</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.35pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 15.35pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 0in;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 26— Power of the Government to seize firearms from the possession of licencee for public safety-Deputy Commissioner has no such power-</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.35pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: 15.35pt; mso-line-height-rule: exactly; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">In exercise of power under section 26 </span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">of the Arms Act, 1878</span>Government can pass order to seize fire arms from the possession of any person including a licensee for public safety. Under section 26 the Deputy Commissioner has no power to pass order to seize fire arms. The position of law being as such the impugned order of the DepLity Commissioner, Mymensingh having been passed without jurisdiction is declared illegal. <em>Golam Ambia Vs. The Deputy Commissioner, Mymensingh and others, 9 MLR (2004) (AD) 134.</em></span></p><p> </p></div></div>
2984	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898</h2>

</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">                                     Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"> </p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 35A— Adjustment of sentence of imprisonment with period of custody before trial—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Special Powers Act, 1974—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Trial Procedure— Section 35A Cr.P.C. not applicable to accused tried by the Special Tribunal—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Special Powers Act is a special law providing special procedure of speedy trial of certain offences and of deterrent punishment. The scheme o the Special Powers Act being as sue the provision of section 35A of the Code of Criminal Procedure shall n be applicable to sentence to imprisonment passed under the Special Powers Act, 1974 for adjustment with the period of custody before trial. <em>Hazrat AH alias Dalai Vs. The State, 10 MLR (2005) (HC) 134.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section 35A— Held not applicable to offence triable under the Special Powers Act, 1974— </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Special Powers Act, 1974— </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 29 and 34B— Exclude the application of other law—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The learned judges of the High Court Division upon interpretation of the provision of section 29 and 34B together with the preamble of the Special Powers Act, 1974 and having regard to the legislative intent and purpose held the concessionary provision of deducting the period of custody from the sentence as contained in section 35A of the Code of Criminal Procedure 1898 not applicable to the case of offence triable under the Special Powers Act, 1974. It is further held that the conviction can well be based on the unimpeachable evidence of police personnel without corroboration by independent public witness. <em>Hiru Miah Vs The State, 10 MLR (2005) (HC) 388.</em></span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898—Section 54— Power of arrest without warrant— Though wide but to be exercised cautiously and not arbitrarily and capriciously</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">—</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Remand— to police custody repeatedly deprecated- Police Official torturing the person in custody is liable to prosecution under section 220 of the Penal Code-Magistrate has to enquire and take appropriate action upon receipt of allegation of torture</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">—</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Police Officers are warned against arbitrary arrest under section 54 of the Code of Criminal Procedure and criminal liability against torture in remand.<em>AM] Md. Yusuf AH Vs. The Slate, </em>7 <em>MLR (2002) (HC) 371.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Sections 61 and 167— Unauthorized detention- legal consequence—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Sections 164 and 364— Confessional statement recorded during unauthorized detention  in police custody- Its voluntariness goes—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">When confessional statements of accused are recorded under section 164 and 364 Cr.P.C. after being produced from police custody for a period more than 48 hours after arrest and that provided under section 61 and 167 Cr.P.C. in the absence of satisfactory explanation, it makes the confessional statement so recorded involuntary and as such can not form the basis of conviction. <em>Belal alias Belial and 2 others Vs. the State, 7 MLR (2002) (HC) 27.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Sections 94 and 160— Power of police officer to direct any person to produce documents and make statement— when can be exercised—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Anti-corruption officer can not direct a person to produce certain documents and make statement unless it is necessary in connection with an enquiry or investigation of a criminal case. Therefore the impugned notice so issued by the Anti-corruption officer not in connection with enquiry or investigation of any criminal case being without any lawful authority is declared illegal. <em>MA. Motalib Vs. Government of Bangladesh, 7 MLR (2002) (HC) 139.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section 94 and 160— Except in connection with inquiry, investiga­tion or trial of a criminal case Police Officer can not direct any person to appear before him to give statement or produce documents</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">—</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"> </p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Neither section 94 nor 160 of the Code of Criminal Procedure 1898 authorizes any police officer to direct any person to appear to give statement or to produce document except in connection with inquiry, investigation or trial of a criminal case. Impugned actions of the Police Officer being highly arbitrary are declared illegal and of no lawful authority. <em>Mohsin Hossain Vs. Government of Bangladesh, 7 MLR (2002) (HC) 182.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section 103— Search and seizure—Evidentiary value of police personnels—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Witnesses of seizure of contrabands recovered through raid on secret information are to be found in the place where the raid is conducted. The manner of seizure in such a case may not conform to the requirements of section 103 Cr.P.C. in its entirety but any such departure does not dininish the evidentiary value of the witness including that of the police personnels of the raiding party. Ground not taken during trial can not be taken in appeal. <em>Trikul Islam Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (HC) 200.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>103—<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>Rule regulating search and seizure—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Since the witnesses are reluctant to depose against their powerful neighbours or habitual miscreants for fear of their life, there is hardly any scope to get local witnesses of seizure and search to give the true evidence during trial. Having regard to the hard realities relating to deteriorating law and order situation, there is no reason not to rely upon the evidence of police personnels of the search and seizure when they appear to be natural and disinterested.<em> Billal Miah Vs. The State, 9 MLR (2004) (HC) 429.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 145- Proceedings for declaring possession of immovable property in dispute—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Magistrate is competent under section 145(4) Cr.P.C. to declare possession of a party to a dispute even <span style="letter-spacing: -.5pt;">if </span>he is found to have been forcibly and wrongfully dispossessed from the proceeding land within two months of <span style="letter-spacing: -.5pt;">the </span>drawing up of the proceedings under section 145(1) Cr.P.C. Proceedings drawn up after two months of dispossession is incompetent and no relief can be given to the aggrieved party in such proceedings. <em>Abdul Kashem Vs. The State and others, </em><span style="letter-spacing: -.5pt;">6 MLR </span><em>(2001) (HC) 90.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section 145— Possession of the petitioner 1st party in the disputed property is the pre-condition of drawing up proceedings</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">—</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Unless the 1st party Petitioner is or has been in possession of the disputed property within 60 days of the initiation of the proceedings, no proceedings under section 145 of the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 can be drawn and the property under dispute cannot be attached and receiver appointed. <em>Kamal Md. Tarik Hossain Vs. Hajee Abul Kashem and others, 7 MLR (2002) (AD) 44.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><em><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code</span></em></strong><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section 145— Findings of possession by the Magistrate based on proper evidence cannot be interfered with— Section 561A— Application doesnot lie against judgment passed in revision.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The High Court Division in exercise of power under section 561A Cr.P.C. cannot interfere with the findings of possession in the disputed land arrived at by the Magistrate on proper appreciation of evidence which has been affirmed by the Additional Sessions Judge in revision.<em>Shamsuddin @ Shamsnddoha and another Vs. Mvi Amjad AH and others, 9 MLR (2004) (AD) 32.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section 145— Oral evidence takes precedence<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>over documentary evidence in respect of possession.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Long lapse of time is not a ground to presume that the apprehension of breach of peace disappeared. As regards proof of possession in disputed property oral evidence lakes precedence over documentary evidence. <em>Saber Ahmed and another Vs. Coura Mia, 8 MLR (2003) (AD) 110.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898—Section 145— When civil court is seized of with the subject matter of dispute regulating possession of the parties therein, the jurisdiction of Magistrate in such matter is ousted— Receiver cannot be appointed without attachment—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The law is well settled that when civil court is in the seisin of the subject-matter of dispute regulating possession of the parties therein, the jurisdiction of Magistrate in such matter tinder section 145 Cr.P.C. is ousted.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Further more receiver can not be appointed without the attachment of the property. In the event of apprehension of breach of peace relating to possession of such property remedy can be sought in the civil court even at the stage of appeal before which it is pending. <em>Abdur Rahman Sikder (Md.) Vs. Nur Mohammad Khan and others, 10 MLR (2005) (HC) 317.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>145(4)— Restoration of possession</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">—</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Proceedings under section 145 of the Code of Criminal Procedure after two months of dispossession are incompetent and as such the Magistrate can not direct restoration of possession which is the well settled position of law. <em>Abul Kashem Vs. Md. Mofizuddin being dead his heirs Halimannessa and others, 6 MLR (2001) (AD) 237.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>154—<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>Second<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>FIR not barred—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Witness— Partisan need not be discarded always unless there is any animus</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">—</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">There is no warrant in law that the partisan witness should always be discarded. Unless there is any animus tending to false implication, the evidence of partisan witness if found consistent and reliable can well form the basis of conviction.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">When during investigation the truth of the prosecution case transpired otherwise than initially informed, there is no bar in law to lodge a fresh or second F.I.R. <em>Abdul Khaleque Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (AD) 170.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section 154 and 161— Delay in lodging F.I.R. and delay in examining witness by the I.O.— legal consequence—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">In a murder case when satisfactorily explained the delay in lodging the F.I.R is not fatal. Similarly when the eye witnesses consistently supported the prosecution case, delay in examination of some witnesses by the Investigating officer does not render any ground to discard their evidence. <em>Golam Azam (Md.) Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (AD) 240.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section 154 and 161— FIR and statement before police—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The G.D. Entry pursuant to which Police took up investigation of the case shall be deemed to be the F.I.R within the meaning of section 154 Cr.P.C. Subsequent F.I.R lodged after the commencement of the investigation shall be deemed to have been statement before police recorded under section 161 Cr.P.C. In view of absence of premeditation for committing the murder, the sentence of death is altered into one of imprisonment for life.<em>Ansar Md. Chan Miah Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (AD) 279.</em></span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>154—<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>FIR<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>is<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>not a substantive piece of evidence— Evidence of related witness cannot be discarded when they appear to be natural—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">It is well settled that FIR is not a substantive piece of evidence. It is important that it gives the information about the occurrence first in point of time and can be<sub>r</sub> taken into consideration to check subsequent embellishment of the prosecution case.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">When the witnesses who are related to each other appear to be natural to the occurrence and their evidences appear to be reliable, such evidence can well form the basis of conviction. When the prosecution appears to have proved the charge by consistent and reliable evidence the conviction and sentence passed by the trial court thereon and affirmed by the lower appellate court are held perfectly justified by the High Court Division. <em>Sohel (Md.) Vs. The State, 10 MLR (2005) (HC) 239.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 154— Delay in lodging FIR is not always fatal for the prosecution—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Delay in lodging FIR when satisfactorily explained is not fatal.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 11— Alibi—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Alibi taken as defence when not proved lends support to the prosecution</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Charges may well be established by circumstantial evidence.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Inquest report is not substantive evidence. In case of conflict the post mortem report prevails over inquest report. <em>State Vs. Md. Ainul Haque, 9 MLR (2004) (HC) 393.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Section 154— First Information Report (FIR)— Evidentiary value of -Inordinate delay when not satisfactorily explained—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Penal Code. 1860—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Section 302— Charge of murder— proof beyond all reasonable doubt—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Sustainability of conviction and sentence when evidence are contradictory and suffer from improbability— Non-appealing convict may be allowed the benefit of acquittal passed in appeal preferred by co-convicts—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">When not satisfactorily explained the inordinate delay in lodging FIR is fatal to the prosecution which casts adverse reflection on the prosecution as to concoction and embellishment. To secure sustainability of conviction and sentence the same must be based on consistent and reliable evidence. The burden of proof entirely lies upon the prosecution to prove the- charge beyond shadow of all reasonable doubt. When the evidence of the ocular witness are sharply contradictory and suffer from legal infirmity and fail far below the level of credence the conviction and sentence passed thereon cannot be sustained in the eye of law. It has now become accepted principle that the benefit of the judgment of acquittal can be extended to non-appealing convicts. <em>Kalam Tahikder and others Vs. The State, 10 MLR (2005) (HC) 264.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Section 154 and 540— FIR distinguished from G.D. entry which does not have the trappings of an F.I.R.— Liability of husband in wife killing case—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Discrepancies and contradictions in evidence are distinguished—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Minor discrepancies which are natural of human behavior are not fatal and do not diminish the evidentiary value as in the case of contradictions.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Where the court considers it essential for proper decision it may summon and examine any witness at any stage of the proceedings before pronouncement of judgment. There is nothing wrong in it where the accused is not prejudiced.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Evidence of witness who is close credence the conviction and sentence passed thereon cannot be sustained in the eye of law. It has now become accepted principle that the benefit of the judgment of acquittal can be extended to non-appealing convicts. <em>Kalam Tahikder and others Vs. The State, 10 MLR (2005) (HC) 264.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Section 154 and 540— FIR distinguished from G.D. entry which does not have the trappings of an F.I.R.— Liability of husband in wife killing case—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Discrepancies and contradictions in evidence are distinguished</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">—</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Minor discrepancies which are natural of human behavior are not fatal and do not diminish the evidentiary value as in the case of contradictions.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Where the court considers it essential for proper decision it may summon and examine any witness at any stage of the proceedings before pronouncement of judgment. There is nothing wrong in it where the accused is not prejudiced.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Evidence of witness who is close relation of the informant and the</span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> deceased can be relied upon unless found biased with animus of enmity.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">When the wife is killed in the custody of the husband, the husband owes liability to explain how his wife died. <em>The State Vs. Nasiruddin (Nasiruddin Vs. State), 9 MLR (2004) (HC) 82.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section 154— First Information Report (FIR)—Its evidentary value when not supported by its maker—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">First Information Report though not a substantive evidence is important to understand the prosecution story at the earliest point of time. It loses its importance when the maker does not support the FIR story. Prosecution has to prove the charge against the accused by legal and consistent evidence. Evidences which are inconsistent and do not inspire confidence cannot form the basis of conviction. <em>Rahima Khatun and another Vs. The State, 9 MLR (2004) (HC) 123.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section 154— F.I.R.—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Delay when not fatal-Delay in lodging the F.I.R. when satisfactorily explained is not fatal for the prosecution. <em>Rakhal Chandra Dey alias Rakhal Chandra Dev Vs. The State, 8 MLR (2003) (AD) 61.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 164— Confessional Statement of Co accused when does not contain incriminating materials can not be ground for refusal of bail of the accused Petitioner</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">—</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">In the absence of any positive evidence corroborating the confessional statement of a co-accused containing incriminating materials against the accused petitioner his prayer for bail cannot be refused. <em>A. Kader Vs. The State, 9 MLR (2004) (HC) 175.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section 164—Confessional statement of accused— Effect of retraction—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">In order to be admissible in evidence forming the basis of conviction, a confessional statement of an accused must be voluntary and true as to the commission of offence. When confessional statement recorded by a Magistrate in accordance with law is found to be voluntary and true, this can be basis of conviction of the confessing accused inspire of the subsequent retraction there from. Where a confessional statement is partly inculpatory and partly exculpatory, this need not be discarded altogether. When the inculpatory part of confession stands supported by other evidence and strong circumstantial evidence, this can well form the basis of conviction against the maker. <em>Shahjahan (Md.) Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (HC) 205.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898—Section 164— Confessional Statement of an accused— </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidentiary value—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 24 and 30— Evidentiary value of confession—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Confessional statement of an accused whether retreated or not can well form the basis of conviction when the Court finds it true and voluntary. Subsequent retraction of confession is of no consequence. <em>Zalal Vs. The State, 7 MLR (2002) (HC)193.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898—Section 164—Confessional statement of accused—Requirement of corroborative evidence—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Confessional statement of an accused when found true and voluntary may well form basis of conviction against the maker thereof. Confessional statement of one accused cannot be relied upon against other co accused without independent corroborative evidence in basing any conviction. <em>Nasir Howladar and others Vs. The State, 7 MLR (2002) (HC) 373.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section 164— Exculpatory confession— Evidentiary value of- Exculpatory confession is no confession in the eye of law and can not form the basis of conviction. <em>AsrafAli @ Sheru Vs. The State, 7 MLR (2002) (HC) 414.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 164— Confessional statement of accused— Not substantive evidence against other accused—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Confessional statement of an accused duly recorded, though not substantive evidence against other accused, it can well be used against that other accused when supported by other evidence direct or circumstantial. <em>The State Vs. Ershad AH Sikdar and others , 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 136.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Investigation- defects- No ground for acquittal—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 4.3pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Informant Police Officer can well investigate the case himself. Accused can not be acquitted only on the grounds of defect in investigation. Where charge is established by reliable evidence, accused can be convicted despite defects in investigation. <em>The State Vs. Ershad Ali Sikdar and others, 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 134.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 (V of 1898)—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 164— Confessional state­ment forming basis of conviction—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 342— Examination of accused and the acceptability of the age stated therein—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Confessional statement of an accused when found true and voluntary can form basis of conviction of the maker. Ascertainment of age is a mixed question of fact and law. Age of an accused as stated during her examination under section 342 Cr.P.C. is more reliable than that disclosed during recording her confessional statement. <em>Khokon Mridha and another Vs. The State, 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 70.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 164— Confessional statement of one accused can not be used against other co accused without independent corroborative evidence—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Penal Code, 1860—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 396— Charge of dacoity with murder- Nature of proof—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Confessional statement of one accused cannot form the sole basis of conviction against other co accused without independent corroborative evidence. Therefore, the conviction based on such confessional statement alone is not sustainable in the eye of law.<em>The State Vs. Md.Tuku Biswas, 8 MLR (2003) (AD) 33.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section 164 and 364— Confessional statement<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">    </span>of accused— Legal requirements for recording— </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act 1872— Section 30— Evidentiary value of confession when retracted— </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Penal Code 1860— Section 302— Motive of murder is immaterial<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>when<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>there<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>is direct evidence—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Confessional statement when found true and voluntary may well form the basis of conviction. Confessional statement partly inculpatory and partly exculpatory may be considered together with other corroborative evidence. Un-explained Police Custody indicates police torture and coercion. Suspicion is no evidence. When there is direct evidence motive of murder is immaterial. In case of retracted confession corroborative evidence will be necessary to SListain conviction. <em>Abdul Qayum Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (HC) 358.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"> </p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.65pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section 164 and 364— Confessional statement of accused—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.65pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Confessional statement both judicial and extra-judicial as to the guilt of the accused when well proved can form the basis of conviction. <em>Azad Mia alias Md. Azad Vs. The State, 7 MLR (2002) (AD) 22.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.65pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898—Section 164— Confessional statement when found voluntary and true can alone form the basis</span></strong> <strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">of conviction against the confessing accused without</span></strong> <strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">corroboration by independent witness—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.65pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 342—Is not an idle formality—Trial Court has duty to explain the incriminating materials on record to the accused while examining him</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">—</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.65pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-bidi-language: BN-BD;"> </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.65pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Madak Drabya Niantran Ain, 1990—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.65pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 19(1)— Possession or control has to be proved beyond all reasonable doubt—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.65pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The cardinal principle of administration of criminal justice is that the prosecution must prove the charge beyond all reasonable doubt.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.65pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Confessional statement when found voluntary and true can form the basis of conviction against the confessing accused without corroboration by independent witness. It is not always safe to convict an accused solely on the basis of interested police personnel.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.65pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Further the trial court has an obligatory duty to explain to the accused the incriminating materials <span style="letter-spacing: .5pt;">on </span>record <span style="letter-spacing: .5pt;">at </span>the time <span style="letter-spacing: .5pt;">of </span>his examination under section <span style="letter-spacing: .5pt;">342 of </span>the Code <span style="letter-spacing: .5pt;">of </span>Criminal Procedure <span style="letter-spacing: .5pt;">to </span>enable him <span style="letter-spacing: .5pt;">to </span>make his defense. Non-compliance with the requirement of section <span style="letter-spacing: .5pt;">342 </span>Cr. P. C. in appropriate manner, vitiates the trial rendering the conviction and sentence un-sustainable in law.</span> <em><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Saiful Islam (Md.) and another Vs. The State, 10 MLR (2005) (HC) 308.</span></em></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.9pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section 164— Confessional statement is not binding upon the co-accused—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.9pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Confessional statement when found true and voluntary can form the basis of conviction against the confessing accused.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.9pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Absconsion of an accused by itself is no evidence of guilt of the absconding accused.<em>Alamgir Hossain (Md.) and another Vs State, 10 MLR (2005) (HC) 357.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.1pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.1pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 164— Confessional Statement forming basis of conviction—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.1pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Nari-O-Shishu Nirjatan Daman (Bishesh Bidhan) Ain, 1995—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.1pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 6(3)— Offence of rape and punishment</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">—</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.1pt 0in .0001pt 13.5pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Confessional statement recorded under section<span style="letter-spacing: .5pt;">164, </span>Cr.P.C. when found</span> <span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">true and voluntary can form the sole basis of conviction against the confessing accused.
