<w:LsdException Locked="false" Priority="39" QFormat="true" Name="TOC Heading"/> </w:LatentStyles></xml><![endif]--><!-- [if gte mso 10]><style> /* Style Definitions */ table.MsoNormalTable	{mso-style-name:"Table Normal";	mso-tstyle-rowband-size:0;	mso-tstyle-colband-size:0;	mso-style-noshow:yes;	mso-style-priority:99;	mso-style-qformat:yes;	mso-style-parent:"";	mso-padding-alt:0in 5.4pt 0in 5.4pt;	mso-para-margin-top:0in;	mso-para-margin-right:0in;	mso-para-margin-bottom:10.0pt;	mso-para-margin-left:0in;	line-height:115%;	mso-pagination:widow-orphan;	font-size:11.0pt;	mso-bidi-font-size:14.0pt;	font-family:"Calibri","sans-serif";	mso-ascii-font-family:Calibri;	mso-ascii-theme-font:minor-latin;	mso-hansi-font-family:Calibri;	mso-hansi-theme-font:minor-latin;}</style><![endif]--></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 10.3pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">                                        Criminal Law Amendment Act, 1958</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 10.3pt;"> </p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 10.3pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 6(5)— Sanction of Government for prosecution—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 10.3pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Penal Code— Section 21— officers and<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>employees of Cooperative Societies are held to be public servants</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">—</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 10.3pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">In view of the provisions of section 2 of the Criminal Law Amendment Act, 1958 read with section 21 of the Penal Code the officers and employees of a Cooperative Society registered under the Cooperative Societies Act, are held to be public servants for whose prosecution sanction of the government under section 197 of the Code of Criminal Procedure is a mandatory pre-requisite without which trial of such person is a nullity and in that view of the matter the case is remanded to the trial court for retrial after obtaining sanction.Iman Ali Mallik (Md.) Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2002) <em>(HC) 162.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 10.3pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Criminal Law Amendment Act, 1958—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 10.3pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 6(5)— Sanction for prosecution of a public servant—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 10.3pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Sanction of the Government for prosecution of a public servant as required under section 197 Cr.P.C. and section 6(5) of the Criminal Law Amendment Act, 1958 is a mandatory prerequisite for prosecution of a public servant. Sanctioning authority must take into consideration the relevant materials constituting the offence and should not act mechanically as a matter of course. Sanction order which is exfacie too mechanical and vitiated by non-application of mind is no sanction in the eye of law and as such the trial held thereon is without jurisdiction consequently rendering the conviction and sentence illegal and not sustainable in law.<em>Syed Mustafizur Rahman Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (HC) 213.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 10.3pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Criminal Law Amendment Act, 1958—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 10.3pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 10— Limitation for appeal against conviction and sentence passed in a case tried in the absence of accused as held by the High Court Division starts from the date of the knowledge about the judgment.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 10.3pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Where in a case the process were not served upon the accused and the case was tried in absentia keeping the accused completely in the dark while staying abroad ending in conviction. The convict-appellant returned home after about 12 years and then filed the appeal within 30 days of knowing about the judgment and order of conviction. The High Court Division considering the peculiar facts and circumstance of the case in relation to the relevant provisions of law admitted the appeal as being one filed within time. <em>Eklimur Reza Khan Vs. The State, 10 MLR (2005) (HC) 323.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 10.3pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Criminal Law Amendment Act, 1958—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 10.3pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 10(4)—Withdrawal of prosecution—Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section 494— Withdrawal of Prosecution<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>with consent of the Court—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 10.3pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Power of the Court where consent of the court is the legal necessity for withdrawal from prosecution has to be exercised Judiciously and not mechanically as contemplated by section 10(4) of the Criminal Law Amendment Act, 1958 read with section 494 of the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898.<em>Loskar Mohammad Mostain Billah Vs. The State, 7 MLR (2002) (HC) 265.</em></span></p></div></div>
2986	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">Cruelty to Women (Deterrent punishment) Ordinance, 1983</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  7 BLT (AD)-143, 9 BLT (HCD)-110, 12 BLT (HCD)446,  12 BLT (AD)-230</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  Cruelty to Women (Deterrent punishment) </p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p style="text-align: center;"> </p><p style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section-4(b)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The age of a victim is not a material consideration for a offence under Section 4(b) of the Cruelty to Women (Deterrent Punishment) Ordinance 1983.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Mozibur Rahman Vs. The State &amp; Anr. 7 BLT (AD)-143</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section-4(b) &amp; 4(c) read with Evidence Act, 1872, Section-114(g)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The only allegation against the accused appellant is that he abated the offence of kidnapping of Habibur Rahman and Booting of money from his room during the occurrence. But none of the P.Ws including the victim Habibur Rahman himself did not state that the accused appellant ever kidnapped him or looted away the money in question. It appears that there is a reservation in the FIR that accused appellant Liakat Ali was with the two other accused persons who kidnapped Habibur Rahman. But during trial save and except a formal statement about fastening of the mouth of the victim Habibur Rahman by Gamsa/towel by the accused appellant there is no other evidence whatsoever in the matter of kidnapping the victim Habibur Rahman or looting of money from his room. P.Ws. 7 and 8 who have categorically said about recovery of the victim Estemera from the nearby house are totally silent about the connection of the accused appellant Liakat Ali. It further appears that none of the close neighours who have admittedly got their houses by the four sides of the P.W. house have not been examined in this case. The non examination of those neighbours in this case together with absence of towel/Gamsa which was allegedly fastened by the accused Liakat in the mouth of the victim Habibur Rahman at the time of his kidnapping has made the entire prosecution case suspicious and in their absence the benefit of Section-114(g) of the Evidence Act must go in favour of the defence.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Mohd. Liakat Ali Vs. The State 9 BLT (HCD)-110</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section-4(C)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Prayer for bail- Accused petitioner is in custody for 31/2 years. Four accused persons have been named in the F. I. R. but excepting the accused- petitioner all the other three accused persons are absconding and there is no possibility of an early disposal of the case- Held: We must observe that if the trial of the four accused persons has still not begun or if there is no immediate possibility of an early disposal of the case the Special Tribunal will have to either split up the case and proceed with the trial of only the accused petitioner or consider a fresh prayer for bail of the accused- petitioner.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Mohammad Sharif Vs. The State 4 BLT (AD)-17</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections-5 read with Penal Code, 1860 Section-457/376/511</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Special Tribunal had only jurisdiction to try cases as -numerated in the schedule of the Special Power Act and not beyond that. An offence under section 457/354/511 of the Penal Code which is not included in the schedule of the Special Powers Act cannot be the basis of conviction as the same is a non-schedule offence.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Dulal Miah @ Shah Alam Vs. The State 12 BLT (HCD)446</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section-6 &amp; 9</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">On perusal of the First Information Report and relevant papers it appears that there is no allegation against the appellant that she abetted the causing or attempted to cause death or grievous hurt to the informant of the case for dowry. The allegation appears to be that she instigated the husband of the informant to demand/realise dowry from the informant -appeal is allowed.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Anowara Begum Vs. The State &amp; Anr 12 BLT (AD)-230</em></strong></p></div></div>
2987	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">Customs Act, 1969</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>   9 MLR (2004) (HC) 252.</p><p><b>Case Year:</b>  1969</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  Civil Law</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  2018-03-21</p><!DOCTYPE html><p><!-- [if gte mso 9]><xml> <o:OfficeDocumentSettings>  <o:RelyOnVML/>  <o:AllowPNG/> </o:OfficeDocumentSettings></xml><![endif]--></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 10.3pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">                                                           Customs Act, 1969</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 10.3pt;"> </p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 10.3pt;"> </p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 10.3pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 15 and 16— Custom Authority is empowered to confiscate goods brought into Bangladesh in contravention of prohibition</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">—</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.85pt 0in .0001pt 10.3pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Since the Customs Authority is empowered to detain and confiscate goods brought into Bangladesh in contravention of prohibition he is not required to obtain permission of the Court or tribunal, as the case may be, for such confiscation pursuant <span style="letter-spacing: -1.0pt;">to</span> the relevant provisions of the Customs Act, 1969.<em>Omar Sharif Khan (Md.) Vs. The State, 9 MLR (2004) (HC) 252.</em></span></p><p> </p></div></div>

—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: The State, 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 422.</em></span></p><p> </p></div></div>
2989	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">Evidence Act, 1872</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  6 MLR (2001) (HC) 237, 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 409, 9 MLR (2004) (HC) 192, 8 MLR(2003)(HC) 1376,  8 MLR (2003) (HC) 137, 9 MLR (2004) (HC) 393, 6 MLR (2001) (HC) 99., 6 MLR (2001) (HC) 358, 10 MLR (2005) (HC) 91, 7 MLR (2002) (AD) 17,  7 MLR (2002) (HC) 20, 7 </p><p><b>Case Year:</b>  1872</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  Civil Law</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  2018-03-21</p><!DOCTYPE html><p><!-- [if gte mso 9]><xml> <o:OfficeDocumentSettings>  <o:RelyOnVML/>  <o:AllowPNG/> </o:OfficeDocumentSettings></xml><![endif]--></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">                                                              Evidence Act, 1872</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 3 and 30</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">— <strong>Evidentiary value of confessional statement—</strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Penal Code, 1860—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 302</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">— <strong>Charge of murder-Legal requirement as to proof of—</strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The well settled principle of law is that the confessional statement of one accused cannot be used against the other co-accused. Moreover the confessional statement recorded under section 30 is not an evidence within the meaning of section 3 of the Evidence Act, 1872. When none of the eyewitnesses of the occurrence said a single word against the convict-appellant, this is factually a case of no evidence. Therefore the impugned order of conviction and sentence which is not based on any legal evidence is set aside with a serious stricture against the learned trial Court judge.Abu <em>Syed Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (HC) 237.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872 —</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 3 and 30- Confessional Statement of an accused is not evidence against the other co-accused—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Confessional statement of one accused cannot be used against the other co-accused without independent corroboration. Circumstantial evidence when unerringly points at the guilt of the accused can form basis of conviction.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">When material witnesses are withheld by the prosecution without satisfactory explanation and the evidence brought on record are contradictory, the prosecution case becomes doubtful leading to the acquittal of the accused. <em>Khokon Vs. The State, 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 409.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 3 and 30</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">— <strong>Evidentiary value of confessional statement of co-accused— Need for corroboration—</strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 133 and 114</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">— <strong>Accomplice is competent witness—</strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Confessional statement of co-accused is not substantive piece of evidence. Again though accomplice is competent witness the rule of prudence insists upon corroboration by other relevant evidence in order to ensure sustainability of conviction based thereon. <em>Ershad AH Sikdar and others Vs. The State, 9 MLR (2004) (HC) 192.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 5.75pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 5.75pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 3 and 114(g)— Adverse presumption against non-examination of material witness— Section 30</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">— <strong>Confessional Statement of co-accused—</strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 5.75pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Absconsion by itself is no ground for conviction. Confessional statement of an accused can not be used against other co accused without independent corroboration. Non-examination of material witness without satisfactory explanation raises adverse presump­tion against prosecution. <em>Emdadul Haque (Md.) @ Raza Mia and others Vs. The State, 8 MLR(2003)(HC) </em>1376.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.8pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872— Section 8 &amp; 9 (c)— Aboscondance of accused- No ground for presumption | of guilt—</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Though abscondance of accused is <strong><span style="mso-font-width: 10%;">I </span></strong>no ground for presumption as to the guilt of the accused, long abscondance lends support to the guilt of the accused. <em>The State Vs. Ershad AH Sikdar and Uthers, 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 137.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872— Section 11— Alibi— Alibi taken during trial— Proof thereof—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 342 Cr.P.C. provides for an opportunity to the accused to explain his position in relation to the evidence brought on record against him. He may file documents which form part of his statement. He is not required to prove those document. Such statement of accused is not evidence within the meaning of section 3 of the Evidence Act, 1872.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Neither the prosecution nor the court can use such statement against the accused except for his benefit. Alibi taken by the accused during trial must be proved by him to the satisfaction of the Court.<em>Minhaz and another Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (AD) 166.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 11— Alibi—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Alibi taken as defence when not proved lends support to the prosecution</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> —</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Charges may well be established by circumstantial evidence.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Inquest report is not substantive evidence.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">In case of conflict the post mortem report prevails over inquest report—<em>State Vs. Md. Ainul Haque, 9 MLR (2004) (HC) 393..</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .5pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872— Section 11</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">— <strong>Acquittal on proof of Alibi-</strong>When satisfactorily established by evidence such as attendance Register that the accused was on duty in his office at the relevant time of the occurrence, the accused may be acquitted on such plea of alibi. <em>Nurul Islam Vs. Abdul Malek and another, 8 MLR (2003) (AD) 37.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 27</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">—<strong>Statement made by an accused in police custody when admissible in evidence—</strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">General rule of evidence is that statement made by an accused in police custody is not admissible in evidence. Section 27 of the Evidence Act is an exception to this rule. Statement of an accused made while in police custody leading to recovery of incriminating material is admissible in evidence. But where there are public eye witnesses of the occurrence who do not support the prosecution case no conviction can be based on the evidence of police personnel only.<em>Ziaur Rahman @ Zillur Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (HC) 99.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act 1872— Section 30</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">— <strong>Evidentiary value of confession when retracted—</strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section 164 and 364</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">— <strong>Confessional statement<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">    </span>of<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>accused</strong>— <strong>Legal requirements for recording—</strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Confessional statement when found true and voluntary may well form the basis of conviction. Confessional statement partly inculpatory and partly exculpatory may be considered together with other corroborative evidence. Un-explained Police Custody indicates police torture and coercion. Suspicion is no evidence. When there is direct evidence motive of murder is immaterial. In case of retracted confession corroborative evidence will be necessary to sustain conviction.Abdul <em>Qaium Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (HC) 358.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.35pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 30</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">— <strong>Confessional statement of accused forming basis of conviction Circumstantial evidence can also form basis of conviction—</strong></span><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Law is well settled that crime need not be always proved by direct ocular evidence. Circumstantial evidence may well form the basis of conviction. Accused can well be convicted on the basis of his confessional statement when found true and voluntary. Confessional statement of an accused cannot be used against other co-accused unless such confessional statement is corroborated by other independent evidence. Circumstantial evidence may also be considered as corroborative evidence. <em>State Vs. Mir Hossain and others, 10 MLR (2005) (HC) 91.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.1pt .95pt .0001pt 0in;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872— </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.1pt .95pt .0001pt 0in;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 32- Dying declaration- Its evidentiary value</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">-</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.1pt .95pt .0001pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Dying declaration has special sanctity as valuable piece of evidence. When a dying declaration as to the cause of death of the victim is accepted by Court as true it can form the sole basis of conviction of the assailant.<em>The State Vs. Abdul Barek and others, 7 MLR (2002) (AD) 17.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 5.75pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 5.75pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 32- Dying declaration-Written or oral- when proved- can form the sole basis of conviction—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 5.75pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The law on dying declaration has by now been well-settled. Dying declaration written or oral when proved and is accepted unhesitatingly by court as true account of the cause of death of the victim can well form the basis of conviction, <em>Sttlifn (Md.) Vs. The State, 7 MLR (2002) (HC) 20.</em></span></p><p class="Style4" style="margin-top: 3.1pt; line-height: normal; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872— </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 5.75pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 32- Dying declaration even oral when established by consistent evidence can form the sole basis of conviction—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 5.75pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Dying declaration oral or documentary has special sanctity. When established as true by consistent evidence oral dying declaration can very well form the sole basis of conviction.<em>The State Vs. Abdul Hatem, 9 MLR (2004) (HC) 228.</em></span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.35pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872— Section 32- Dying declaration-Evidentiary value for conviction—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.35pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Dying declaration has a special sanctity in view of the fact that a dying man as expected cannot tell lie and as such a dying declaration when considered true can alone form the basis of conviction.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.35pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Order of acquittal which is perverse and is based on findings on improper appreciation of evidence need be set aside. The High Court Division in such a case is required to interfere with the order of acquittal which is shockingly unconscionable.<em> The State Vs. Rashid Ahmed and others, 7 MLR (2002) (HC) 147.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 114(g)— Adverse presump­tion when can be drawn- Non-examination of charge sheeted witness—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">There is no hard and fast rule that each and every charge sheeted witness must be examined by the prosecution. What is required is that the prosecution must prove the charge with cogent, consistent and reliable evidence on record beyond doubt. Non-examination of any or some charge sheeted witness not vital by itself is no ground for drawing adverse presumption against the prosecution. <em>Rakhal Chandra </em>Dey alias <em>Rakhal Chandra Dev Vs. The State, 8 MLR (2003) (AD) 61.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872— Section 114(g)— Presumption of non-examination of vital witness—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Non-examination of vital witness raises presumption adverse against the prosecution resulting in acquittal of the accused. <em>Rahima Khatun and another Vs. The State, 9 MLR (2004) (HC) 123.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872— Section 118— Evidence of child witness is admissible—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The cardinal principle of criminal jurisprudence is that the prosecution must prove the charge against the accused with consistent and reliable evidence beyond all reasonable doubt. When material evidence are withheld and the evidence brought on record are inconsistent and the charge does not stand proved to the hilt beyond all reasonable<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>doubt<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>the<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>accused is entitled to be acquitted on benefit of doubt. Such doubt must not be imaginary or fanciful. <em>State Vs. Abul Kashem. 10 MLR (2005) (HC) 164.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 133 and 114(b)-Accomplice is competent witness-corroboration by independent evidence when can be dispensed with—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 133 of the Evidence Act, 1872 provides that an accomplice is a competent witness and conviction may be based on the sole evidence of accomplice when found trustworthy. Section 114(b) provides for precaution insisting upon corroboration by independent evidence. But in appropriate case the court in its discretion may dispense with the requirement of corroboration. <em>The State Vs. Ershad Ali Sikdar <strong><span style="letter-spacing: .5pt; mso-font-width: 66%;">and </span></strong>others, 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 132.</em></span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence of Partisan witness—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence of a witness can not be rejected on the ground of his being partisan. Evidence of partisan witness when found reliable can well form the basis of conviction. <em>The State Vs. Ershad Ali Sikdar <strong><span style="mso-font-width: 60%;">and </span></strong>others, 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 134.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 134- No particular number of witness is necessary to prove the charge—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Law does not prescribe for any particular number of witness to prove the charge. Conviction may be awarded on the basis of evidence of solitary witness if found reliable. <em>The State Vs. Ershad AH Sikdar and others, 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 134.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Minor discrepancies in evidence may be ignored—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Acquittal on ground of m tor discrepancies in evidence of witness is not permissible which should be ignored. <em>The State Vs. Ershad AH Sikdar and others, 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 135.</em></span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Benefit of doubt- as ground for acquittal—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">To be a ground for acquittal benefit of doubt must be reasonable and should not be imaginary and fanciful. <em>The State Vs. Ershad Ali Sikdar and others, 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 135.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Part of the evidence disbelieved-Remaining part may be accepted—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">There is no legal bar to accept part of the evidence where the other part is disbelieved. <em>The State Vs. Ershad AH Sikdar and others, 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 135.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 134- No particular number of witness is necessary to prove any particular charge. Evidence to be weighed but not counted—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Cruelty to Women (Deterrent Punishment) Ordinance, 1983—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 4(c)— Punishment for offence of rape—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Penal Code, I860—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 376- Punishment for offence of rape— Alteration of conviction and sentence from section 4(c) of the Ordinance 1983 to section 376 of the Penal Code—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">In a case which was tried by the Special Tribunal and the accused was convicted and sentenced for offence of rape and when subsequently it was found that the offence should have been tried by the Session Judge where the same person presiding over both the Session Court and Tribunal, the conviction and sentence has been altered from section 4(c) of the Ordinance, 1983 into one under section 376 of the Penal Code having regard to the legislative intent and purpose instead allowing the ends of justice frustrated by technicalities.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">It is further held by the High Court Division that conviction and sentence can well be based on the solitary evidence of the prosecutrix if it inspires confidence of the Court.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 134 of the Evidence Act, 1872 does not provide for any particular number of witness to prove any particular charge, the golden rule of evidence being that evidence has to be weighed but not counted. <em>Shibu Pada Acharjee Vs. The State, 8 MLR (2003) (HQ 275.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 134— Evidence of Solitary witness can form basis of conviction—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Long Absconsion of accused immediate after occurence is important circumstantial evidence</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">—</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Golden rule of evidence is that conviction may be based on evidence of solitary witness when found trustworthy. <em>Wasim Mia (Md.) and another Vs. The State, 9 MLR (2004) (HC) 162</em></span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> <br/></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 134— Conviction may be awarded on the basis of evidence of a solitary witness if found trustworthy—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">It is well within the competence of the trial court to convict an accused for the offence proved during trial although he was not specifically charged with under the relevant section of the law as contemplated under section 236 and 237 of the Code of Criminal Procedure. Confessional statement-judicial or extra-judicial when found true and voluntary can well form basis of conviction. Prosecution is not bound to produce each and every witness mentioned in the charge sheet. Abettor is also liable to the same sentence as the principal offender. <em>State Vs. Md. Abdns Samad Azad and another, 10 MLR (2005) (HC) 46.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.55pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Evidence Act, 1872— Circumstantial evidence—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Generally in criminal trial the accused has no liability to prove his innocence. Prosecution has to prove the charge against him beyond shadow of all reasonable doubt in order to secure conviction. But in a wife killing case when both the husband and the deceased wife were living in the same house at the time of the occurrence the husband has heavy liability to explain the circumstance under which his wife met with her death. When the explanation offered is found false, the circumstantial evidence strong and inconsistent with the innocence of the accused husband unerringly pointing at his guilt can safely form the basis of his conviction.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .05in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Since the Sessions Judge is also the Judge of the Nari-O-Shishu Nirjaton Daman Adalat the sentence passed under section 11 (Ka) of the Ain of 2000 can be altered into one under section 302 of the Penal Code when the commission of the murder is proved to have been caused as one unrelated with demand for dowry.<em>State Vs. Kamrnzzaman alias Mantii, 10 MLR (2005) (HC) 346.</em></span></p><p> </p></div></div>

style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;"> </span>Section 41— Offence relating to forest timber—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Charge of illegal possession of forest timber has to be established with reliable and consistent evidence. Inordinate delay in lodging FIR without satisfactory explanation makes the prosecution version doubtful. <em>Mobarakullah (Md.) and another Vs. The State, 9 MLR (2004) (HC) 208.</em></span></p></div></div>
2991	<h2 class="blog_entry-title"> Limitation Act, 1908</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  9 MLR (2004) (AD) 164</p><p><b>Case Year:</b>  1908</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  Civil Law</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b> Roman','serif';">                                                                   Limitation Act, 1908</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"> </p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Applicability of article 157</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">— <strong>as to the limitation against acquittal in case started on police report—</strong></span></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Times New Roman'; mso-fareast-theme-font: minor-fareast; mso-ansi-language: EN-US; mso-fareast-language: EN-US; mso-bidi-language: BN;">In case of appeal against acquittal in a complaint case the limitation is 60 days as provided under section 417(1)(3) of the Code of Criminal Procedure. But in case of appeal against acquittal in a case initiated on police report the limitation is 6 months under article 157 of the Limitation Act. <em>Dr. M.A. Mazed and others Vs. Government of Bangladesh represented by Solicitor, 9 MLR (2004) (AD) 164</em></span></p></div></div>

class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">                                              Madak Drabya Niantran Ain, 1990</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 19(1)— Possession or control has to be proved beyond all reasonable doubt</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">—</span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The cardinal principle of criminal justice is that the prosecution must <em>prove the charge </em>beyond all reasonable doubt.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Confessional statement when found voluntary and true can form the basis of conviction against the confessing accused without corroboration by independent witness. It is not always safe to convict an accused solely on the basis of evidence of interested police personnel.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Further the trial court has an obligatory duty to explain to the accused the incriminating materials on record at the time of his examination under section 342 of the Code of Criminal Procedure to enable him to make his defence. Non-compliance with the requirement of section 342 Cr. P. C. in appropriate manner, vitiates the trial rendering the conviction and sentence un-sustainable in law. <em>Saiful Islam (Md.) and another Vs. The State, 10 MLR (2005) (HC) 308.</em></span><!-- [if gte mso 9]><xml> <o:OfficeDocumentSettings>  <o:RelyOnVML/>  <o:AllowPNG/> </o:OfficeDocumentSettings></xml><![endif]--></p><p class="MsoNormal" class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Motive in murder trial—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Prosecution is not required to prove motive always in murder trial. But when motive is offered the prosecution has to prove motive in order to bring home the charge against the accused.<em>The State Vs. Abdul Hatem. 9 MLR (2004) (HC) 228.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Motive of murder—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">When the charge is established by direct evidence prosecution is not required to prove the motive. But in a case of circumstantial evidence motive has to be proved. <em>The State Vs. Ershad AH Sikdar and others, 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 133.</em></span></p><p> </p></div></div>
2993	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">Narcotics Control Act, 1990</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p></p><p style='margin-left:0in;text-align:justify;text-justify:inter-ideograph;Cambria","serif";'> </p><p style='margin-left:0in;text-align:center;Cambria","serif";'><b>Narcotics Control Act, 1990</b></p><p style='margin-left:0in;text-align:justify;text-justify:inter-ideograph;Cambria","serif";'> </p><p style='margin-left:0in;text-align:justify;text-justify:inter-ideograph;Cambria","serif";'>Section 19(3)- Offence of possessionof ganja plants held not proved when the prosecution led no evidence as to theland where from the ganja plants recovered belongs to the convict<b><span style='Calibri","sans-serif";'> petitioner</span></b><b><span style='Cambria","serif";'>.</span></b></p><p style='margin-left:0in;text-align:justify;text-justify:inter-ideograph;Cambria","serif";'>Convict-petitioner was convicted forthe offence <span style='Calibri","sans-serif";'>under section</span><span style='Cambria","serif";'>19(3) the </span><span style='Calibri","sans-serif";'>narcotics </span><span style='Cambria","serif";'>Control Act, 1990 and the </span><span style='Calibri","sans-serif";'>conviction</span><span style='Cambria","serif";'> and sentence were also affirmed by theSessions Judge in Appeal. The learned </span><span style='font-size:14.0pt;Calibri","sans-serif";'>judge</span><span style='Cambria","serif";'> Of the High </span><span style='Calibri","sans-serif";'>court</span><span style='line-height:115%;Cambria","serif";'>Division set aside </span><span style='Calibri","sans-serif";'>the</span><span style='Cambria","serif";'> order of conviction and sentence inview of </span><span style='Calibri","sans-serif";'>absense</span><span style='Cambria","serif";'> of evidence on record showing thatthe land wherefrom the ganja plants were recovered actually belongs to theconvict-petitioner.</span></p><p style='margin-left:0in;text-align:justify;text-justify:inter-ideograph;Cambria","serif";'>Shamsher All (Md.) Vs. The State 12MLR <span style='Cambria","serif";'>(2007)<i>(HC) 38.</i></span></p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span> </div></div>

Roman','serif';">                                                   Narcotics Control Act, 1990 <br/></span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 10.55pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"> </p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 10.55pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 19(l)/6(kha)- Acceptability of the evidence of police personnel—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 10.55pt; text-align: justify;"><span style="font-size: 11pt; font-family: times new roman,times,serif;">In the face of practical experience arising out of the prevailing situation of the country where the local witnesses are mostly failed to support the prosecution case out of sense of insecurity, the evidence of police personnel’s who appeared natural to the recovery and seizure of contraband goods may well be relied upon in appropriate case without corroboration by the local witness as there is no inflexible rule of law for always disbelieving the evidence of police personnels. Billal<em> Mia Vs. The State, 6 MLR (200m (HC) 33.</em></span></p></div></div>
