<w:LsdException Locked="false" Priority="72" SemiHidden="false"   UnhideWhenUsed="false" Name="Colorful List Accent 6"/>  <w:LsdException Locked="false" Priority="73" SemiHidden="false"   UnhideWhenUsed="false" Name="Colorful Grid Accent 6"/>  <w:LsdException Locked="false" Priority="19" SemiHidden="false"   UnhideWhenUsed="false" QFormat="true" Name="Subtle Emphasis"/>  <w:LsdException Locked="false" Priority="21" SemiHidden="false"   UnhideWhenUsed="false" QFormat="true" Name="Intense Emphasis"/>  <w:LsdException Locked="false" Priority="31" SemiHidden="false"   UnhideWhenUsed="false" QFormat="true" Name="Subtle Reference"/>  <w:LsdException Locked="false" Priority="32" SemiHidden="false"   UnhideWhenUsed="false" QFormat="true" Name="Intense Reference"/>  <w:LsdException Locked="false" Priority="33" SemiHidden="false"   UnhideWhenUsed="false" QFormat="true" Name="Book Title"/>  <w:LsdException Locked="false" Priority="37" Name="Bibliography"/>  <w:LsdException Locked="false" Priority="39" QFormat="true" Name="TOC Heading"/> </w:LatentStyles></xml><![endif]--><!-- [if gte mso 10]><style> /* Style Definitions */ table.MsoNormalTable	{mso-style-name:"Table Normal";	mso-tstyle-rowband-size:0;	mso-tstyle-colband-size:0;	mso-style-noshow:yes;	mso-style-priority:99;	mso-style-qformat:yes;	mso-style-parent:"";	mso-padding-alt:0in 5.4pt 0in 5.4pt;	mso-para-margin-top:0in;	mso-para-margin-right:0in;	mso-para-margin-bottom:10.0pt;	mso-para-margin-left:0in;	line-height:115%;	mso-pagination:widow-orphan;	font-size:11.0pt;	mso-bidi-font-size:14.0pt;	font-family:"Calibri","sans-serif";	mso-ascii-font-family:Calibri;	mso-ascii-theme-font:minor-latin;	mso-hansi-font-family:Calibri;	mso-hansi-theme-font:minor-latin;}</style><![endif]--></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">                                              Prevention of Corruption Act, 1947</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 5A- Investigation by Assistant Inspector of BAC does not render the proceedings illegal</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">From the reading of section 5A of the Prevention of Corruption Act, 1947 and section 3(2) of the Anti-Corruption Act, 1957 and paragraph 59 of Anti-Corruption Manual, it appears that investigation of offence under the Prevention of Corruption Act, 1947 by the Assistant Inspector, BAC is not without jurisdiction. <em>Government of Bangladesh represented by Solicitor, Ministry of Law, Justice and Parliamentary Affairs Vs. Md. Amjad Ali Mridha and others, 9 MLR (2004) (AD) 231.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The Prevention of Corruption Act, 1947—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">(Act No. II of 1947)—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 5(1) (2)— Criminal intention is the prerequisite—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">In the absence of criminal intention no offence punishable under section 5(2) of the Prevention of Corruption Act is committed. When certain acts are done collectively following the rules of procedure, no criminal intention can be inferred. Therefore when no offence is constituted the criminal proceedings initiated thereon being abuse of the process of the court is liable to the quashed. <em>Begum Khaleda Zia Vs. The State, 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 308.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Prevention of Corruption Act, 1947—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 5(2)</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">— <strong>Criminal misconduct-constituent elements—</strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">When the allegations do not constitute offence under section 418 of the Penal Code read with section 5(2) of the Prevention of Corruption Act the<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>proceedings<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>initiated thereon being abuse of the process of the court are quashed. <em>Mohammad Nasim and another Vs. The State, 9 MLR (2004) (HC) 364.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Prevention of Corruption Act, 1947—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 5(l)(d) and 5(l)(e)—Conviction and sentence for criminal misconduct and for possession of pecuniary resources disproportionate to his known sources of income</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">—</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Constitution of Bangladesh— Article 103(3)— Special Leave to appeal when not granted but sentence is modified—</span></strong><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">When the evidence in the chain of events are abundantly clear and consistent showing the abuse of official power by public servant the conviction and sentence are held to be justified and the leave petition is rejected with the modification of sentence and with observation that mere technicalities of non-elaboration of evidence which did not prejudice the convict-appellant will not serve any useful purpose if the leave is granted. <em>Lt. General (Retd.) H.M. Ershad Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (AD) 11.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Prevention of Corruption Act, 1947—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 5(2)— Criminal misconduct and punishment— Reduction of sentence—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The convict-appellant an office peon having been found to have been acted in connivance with others in creating false appointment letters and convicted and sentenced to suffer three years R.I. and pay fine and on compassionate view the sentence was reduced to the term already served with the remissions of fine, such an order does not suffer from any illegality calling for any Interference. <em>Abdul Latif Miah (Md.) Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (AD) 192.</em></span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Prevention of Corruption Act, 1947—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 5(2)— Criminal mis­conduct— conviction and sentence— when charge well proved— No interference is called for- Advocate's conduct is deprecated for misleading submission—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">When conviction and sentence against the convict-appellant who were public servants at the time of commission of offence are well established by consistent evidence on record such conviction and sentence do not call for any interference. Advocate's conduct for misleading submission that the convict appellants were not public servants has been deprecated. <em>Abdul Bari and another Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (AD) 55.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Prevention of Corruption Act. 1947—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 5(2)-</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> Criminal misconduct in relation to misappropriation under section 409 of the Penal Code-In a case of criminal misconduct read with misappropriation under section 409 of the Penal Code where the charge stands proved, the substantive sentence is remitted on depositing the amount of fine only on compassionate ground of health problem by the apex court. <em>Mohibnl Islam Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (AD) </em>79.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Prevention of Corruption Act, 1947—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 5(2)— Offence of criminal misconduct by abuse of official position—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">In a case where the Minister and the Secretary acted merely on the recommendation of tender committee, they are not liable for any commission of offence, if any, committed by the members of the tender committee. When no offence is disclosed either in the FIR or in the charge sheet, the proceedings initiated thereon being abuse of the process of the court are held by the apex court to have been rightly quashed by the High Court Division. <em>State Vs. Mohammad Nasim and others, 10 MLR (2005) (AD) 213.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Prevention of Corruption Act, 1947—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 5(2)— Punishment for criminal misconduct—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">To constitute offence punishable under section 409/109 of the Penal Code read with section 5(2) of the Prevention of Corruption Act, 1947, there must be entrustment and dishonest misappropriation of property by abuse of the official power. Mere doing some routine work by way of making entry in cash book of certain amount by the Secretary of Union Parishad under direction of the chairman who enchased the cheque and the money was never entrusted to the convict appellant and there being no evidence of complicity and involvement with the alleged misappropriation or abetment thereof on the part of the convict appellant, the conviction and sentence has been held by the High Court Division as not sustainable in law.<em>Moslem Uddin (Md.) Vs. The State, 10 MLR (2005) (HC) 199.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Prevention of Corruption Act, 1947—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 5(2)— Criminal Misconduct by Public Servant—</span></strong></p><p class="Style2" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Charge of misappropriation under section 409 of the Penal Code read with section 5(2) of the Prevention of Corruption Act, 1947 has to be established with consistent and cogent evidence in order to secure conviction of accused public servant. In the case of misappropriation in relation to earth work the measurement book is the relevant evidence to establish the charge. When the measurement book is not produced and the evidence so brought on record are discrepant, no conviction in such a case is held to be sustainable. <em>Zahirul Haq (Md.) and another Vs. The State, 7 MLR (2002) (HC) 458.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Prevention of Corruption Act, 1947— </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 5(2)</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">—<strong>Criminal misconduct— constituent elements—</strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">When the allegations do not constitute offence under section 418 of the Penal Code read with section 5(2) of the Prevention of Corruption Act the proceedings initiated thereon being abuse of the process of the court are quashed. <em>Mohammad Nasim and another Vs. The State, 9 MLR (2004) (HC) 364.</em></span></p></div></div>

font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">—<strong>Offence relating to public examination and punishment therefore—Circumstantial evidence— when determining factor—</strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Appropriate mode of assessment of evidence of witness—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Both the examination in chief and cross-examination should be taken equally for evaluation. No finding based either on portion of examination of chief by ignoring the portion of cross-examination is not sustainable in law. Circumstantial evidence at times may be determining factor as to the finding of guilt or otherwise of the accused. Tomal <em>Biswas Vs. The State 6 MLR (2001) (HC) 340.</em></span></p></div></div>

life is modified into one for 10 years R.I.—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Convict-petitioner did not prefer appeal against the conviction and sentence within the statutory period of limitation. Thereafter he filed an application under section 561A Cr.P.C. for quashment of the conviction and sentence which was rejected. Appellate Division while disposing of the petition for leave to appeal modified the sentence of imprisonment for life into one for rigorous imprisonment for 10(ten) years. <em>Jahangir Alam (Md.) alias Zakir Vs. The State, 10 MLR (2005) (AD) 335.</em></span></p></div></div>
3004	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">Special Powers Act, 1974 </h2>

<w:LsdException Locked="false" Priority="39" QFormat="true" Name="TOC Heading"/> </w:LatentStyles></xml><![endif]--><!-- [if gte mso 10]><style> /* Style Definitions */ table.MsoNormalTable	{mso-style-name:"Table Normal";	mso-tstyle-rowband-size:0;	mso-tstyle-colband-size:0;	mso-style-noshow:yes;	mso-style-priority:99;	mso-style-qformat:yes;	mso-style-parent:"";	mso-padding-alt:0in 5.4pt 0in 5.4pt;	mso-para-margin-top:0in;	mso-para-margin-right:0in;	mso-para-margin-bottom:10.0pt;	mso-para-margin-left:0in;	line-height:115%;	mso-pagination:widow-orphan;	font-size:11.0pt;	mso-bidi-font-size:14.0pt;	font-family:"Calibri","sans-serif";	mso-ascii-font-family:Calibri;	mso-ascii-theme-font:minor-latin;	mso-hansi-font-family:Calibri;	mso-hansi-theme-font:minor-latin;}</style><![endif]--></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">                                                  Special Powers Act, 1974 </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 30(I)(a)— Detention <span style="mso-spacerun: yes;"> </span>ordered by the Government— Grounds to be served upon the detenu—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Government has the power under section 3(1)(a) of the Special Powers Act, 1974 to issue detention order for 90 days more but in so doing it shall have to supply the grounds to the detenu as contemplated under section 8 of the Act. <em>Hedayet Ali on behalf of Md. Abbas Ali Vs. Government of Bangladesh represented by Secretary Ministry <span style="letter-spacing: 1.5pt;">of </span>Home Affairs, 7 MLR (2002) (HC) 427.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Special Powers Act, 1974— Section 3(l)(a) and 3(2)— Power of the Government and District Magistrate to order detention.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Government has power tinder section 3(1)(a) of the Special Powers Act, 1974 to pass independently order of detention in order to prevent any person from doing any prejudicial act. But at the time of issuing the detention order the detenu must be supplied with the grounds of detention.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">When grounds of detention are not supplied as required by section 8 of the Act the detention order becomes illegal and denude of any legal effect warranting release of the detenu. When criminal case is started for certain offence, the accused cannot be detained under the Special Powers Act for the same. <em>Nazrul Islam Vs. The State and others, 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 222.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The Special Powers Act, 1974</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> (Act XIV of 1974)—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 3(2)— Detention should be on reasonable ground— Compensatory cost awarded in case of capricious detention.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Power of the District Magistrate to order detention of a person for prejudicial act under section 3(2) of the Special Powers Act, 1974 is extra­ordinary which should be exercised judiciously and not whimsically and capriciously. <em>Korban (Md.) Vs. Government of Bangladesh and others, 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 179</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Special Powers Act, 1974— </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Section 3(2)— Detention-validity-vagueness of grounds— </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">G.D. Entry on vague allegations alone can not be the basis <strong>of </strong>detention-Detention in curtailment of fundamental rights on vague and unspecified allegations based on a G.D. entry in denial of opportunity of proper representation and its continuance for further period when the detenu has already been granted bail in a regular case started in pursuance of the said G.D. entry are held to be illegal. <em>Alamgir alias Alam Vs. The State, 6 </em>MLR (2001) (HC) 133.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Special Powers Act, 1974 — </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Section 3(2)—Detention for prejudicial acts—Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>491— Writ of Habeas corpus— Constitution of Bangladesh— Article 102 (2) (b) (i)— Infringement of fundamental rights</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">—</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Under section 491 of the Code either the detenu himself or a third person on his behalf can invoke the jurisdiction of the High Court Division challenging the legality of detention. But under article 102(2)(b)(i) of the Constitution only the aggrieved person may apply.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">District Magistrate or Additional District Magistrate has no jurisdiction to order detention of a person in the Metropolitan Area where the Chief Metropolitan Magistrate can pass such order. The authority while ordering detention must act judicially and not arbitrarily. <em>Anwar Hossain (Md.) and others Vs. The State and others, 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 385.</em></span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';"> </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Special Powers Act, 1974—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Section 3 and 8(2)— Detention— Grounds for detention to be supplied within 15 days— Effect of order of detention</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">—</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">As soon as the order of detention is signed by the District Magistrate it takes effect. Grounds for detention shall have to be supplied to the detenu within 15 days of making the order as mandated under sub-section (2) of Section 8 of the Special Powers Act, 1974 faihire of which renders the order of detention illegal. <em>Habibur Rahman Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (HC) 311.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Special Powers Act, 1974— </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Schedule- Offence—Joint trial of Schedule and non-schedule offence—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Arial Unicode MS';">Offences included in the Schedule of the Special Powers Act, 1974 are exclusively triable by Special Tribunal.</span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> Joint trial<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>of<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>schedule<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>and non-schedule offence is not permissible <em>Batnl Rabidas and another Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (HC) 349.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Special Powers Act, 1974— </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 25A(b)— Offence of counterfeiting currency note—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Knowledge about the currency as counterfeit and means rea and attempt to use the same are the necessary ingredients which constitute the offence punishable under section 25A(b) of the Special Powers Act, 1974. When these essential ingredients are not established by the prosecution, the convict-appellant as village house wife is acquitted of the charge despite the fact of recovery of counterfeit currency from her possession. <em>Omela Bibi Vs. The State, 7 MLR (2002) (HC) 84.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Special Powers Act, 1974—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 25(b}(2)— Offence of Smuggling— Proof of contrabands—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Chemical examination of phensydil</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">—</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">When it is not shown that the seized articles were contraband goods and when the prosecution evidence are sharply contradictory on material points, conviction of the convict-appellant can not be sustained in the eye of law. Non-appealing convict may also get the benefit of acquittal when the whole prosecution case is found to have been not proved. <em>Rajn Ahmed and others Vs. The State, 7 MLR (2002) (HC) 112.</em></span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Special Powers Act, 1974— </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 27— Special Tribunal to take cognizance upon police report —</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 200 and 190— Power of Magistrate to direct investigation by police</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">—</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">It is not correct to say that the Magistrate who has power to take cognizance can only direct the police to investigate into offence. But in a case where the magistrate has no power to try the offence he' can send the complaint to the police officer for investigation without examining the complainant under section 200 Cr.P.C. Where the Magistrate examines the complainant Linder section 200 Cr.P.C. and then sends the complaint to the police for investigation, this is an irregularity curable under section 537 Cr.P.C. Where upon receipt of police report in writing the Special Tribunal took cognizance of the offence complained of as in the instant case, the proceedings do not suffer from any legal infirmity warranting quashment thereof. <em>Golam Rahman (Md.) Vs. Bazlur Rahman and another, 6 MLR (2001) (HC) 232.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Special Powers Act, 1974—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 27(6)— Trial of accused in absentia— Mandatory requirements of law as to publication of proclamation in news papers—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Cruelty to Women (Deterrent Punishment) Ordinance 1983—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 4 and 9 — Offence are triable by Special Tribunal—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">As provided under sub-section (6) of Section 27 of the Special Powers Act, 1974, the Special Tribunal shall publish in at least two daily news papers proclamation directing appearance of absconding accused before the tribunal on a date fixed. Without such publication the trial will be illegal and without jurisdiction. Special Tribunal can not act on the notification published by Magistrate under section 339B of the Cr.P.C. In every such case service of P &amp; A under section 87 and 88 of the Code of Criminal Procedure shall be equally necessary for providing the reasons for the satisfaction of the Tribunal For holding trial of the accused in absentia. <em>Samsul Alam Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (HC) 297.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Special Powers Act, 1974— </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 27(6)— Trial in absentia—Legal requirements— Accused in custody cannot be tried in absentia—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Special Tribunal himself has to direct by specific order publications in two daily newspapers requiring the appearance of the accused on certain date. But the accused who is in custody in connection with other case cannot be tried in absentia treating him as absconder. Such a trial being illegal and without jurisdiction the conviction and sentence are set aside with direction for retrial in accordance with law. <em>Monir and Shamim Vs. The State, 6 MLR (2001) (HC) 301.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Special Powers Act, 1974— Section 29 and 34B— Exclude the application of other law—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The learned judges of the High Court Division upon interpretation of the provision of section 29 and 34B together with the preamble of the Special Powers Act, 1974 and having regard to the legislative intent and purpose held the concessionary provision of deducting the period of custody from the sentence as contained in section 35A of the Code of Criminal Procedure 1898 not applicable to the case of offence triable under the Special Powers Act, 1974. It is further held that the conviction can well be based on the unimpeachable evidence of police personnel without corroboration by independent public witness. <em>Hint Miah Vs The State, 10 MLR (2005) (HC) 388.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Special Powers Act, 1974— Section 30—Appeal against judgment and order of conviction and sentence—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Order of conviction and sentence which is not based on evidence on record is no judgment and order in the eye of law and the Tribunal has no jurisdiction to pass any such order of conviction and sentence. In a case where the Special Tribunal passed judgment and order of conviction and sentence without any legal evidence on record, such order of conviction and sentence can be declared illegal and passed without jurisdiction in writ petition although the petitioner could not prefer appeal against the same under section 30 of the Special Powers Act, 1974 in the circumstance beyond control. <em>Alam Hossain @ Ibrahim Vs. Government of Bangladesh and others, 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 13.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Special Powers Act, 1974—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Trial Procedure—Section 35A Cr.P.C. not applicable to accused tried by the Special Tribunal—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Special Powers Act is a special law providing special procedure for speedy trial of certain offences and for deterrent punishment. The scheme of the Special Powers Act being as such the provision of section 35A of the Code of Criminal Procedure shall not be applicable to sentence of imprisonment passed under the Act, 1974 for adjustment with the period of custody before trial. <em>Hazrat AH alias Dulal Vs. The State, 10 MLR (2005) (HC) 134.</em></span></p></div></div>

class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">                                                           Speedy Trial Tribunal Act, 2002</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 5 and 6- Not ultravires the Constitution—</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The Speedy Trial Tribunal Act, 2002 being an enactment "for providing speedy trial as contemplated by article 35 of the constitution, section 5 and 6 of the Ain are not violative of the article 27 and 35 of the Constitution. However considering the hardship of the accused the court directed the trial of the accused to be held in the headquarters of their own district. <em>Abdul Kader Mirza and Dabirul Alam @ Masum Vs. Bangladesh represented <span style="letter-spacing: -1.0pt;">by</span> the Secretary Ministry of Law, Justice and Parliamentary Affairs and others, 8 MLR (2003) (HC) 404.</em></span></p></div></div>
